title="UTC+01:00">UTC+1</a>)</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Somerset</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/\xca\x8c/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cut&#39;">\xca\x8c</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/\xc9\x99r/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">\xc9\x99r</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-Somerset.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/En-uk-Somerset.ogg" class="internal" title="En-uk-Somerset.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span> or locally <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/\xca\x8c/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cut&#39;">\xca\x8c</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/\xc9\x99r/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">\xc9\x99r</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Archaism" title="Archaism">archaically</a>, <b>Somersetshire</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_counties_of_England" title="Ceremonial counties of England">county</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_West_England" title="South West England">South West England</a> which borders <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Wiltshire" title="Wiltshire">Wiltshire</a> to the east, <a href="/wiki/Dorset" title="Dorset">Dorset</a> to the south-east and <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> to the south-west. It is bounded to the north and west by the <a href="/wiki/Severn_Estuary" title="Severn Estuary">Severn Estuary</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Channel" title="Bristol Channel">Bristol Channel</a>, its coastline facing southeastern <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>. Its traditional border with Gloucestershire is the <a href="/wiki/River_Avon_(Bristol)" class="mw-redirect" title="River Avon (Bristol)">River Avon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rajan_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rajan-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Somerset\'s <a href="/wiki/County_town" title="County town">county town</a> is <a href="/wiki/Taunton" title="Taunton">Taunton</a>.\n</p><p>Somerset is a rural county of rolling hills, the <a href="/wiki/Blackdown_Hills" title="Blackdown Hills">Blackdown Hills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mendip_Hills" title="Mendip Hills">Mendip Hills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quantock_Hills" title="Quantock Hills">Quantock Hills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exmoor" title="Exmoor">Exmoor National Park</a>, and large flat expanses of land including the <a href="/wiki/Somerset_Levels" title="Somerset Levels">Somerset Levels</a>. There is evidence of human occupation from <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Britain" title="Prehistoric Britain">Paleolithic</a> times, and of subsequent settlement by the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Romans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxons</a>. The county played a significant part in <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred the Great</a>\'s rise to power, and later the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Monmouth_Rebellion" title="Monmouth Rebellion">Monmouth Rebellion</a>. The city of <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a> is famous for its <a href="/wiki/Georgian_era" title="Georgian era">Georgian</a> architecture and is a UNESCO <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Toponymy"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Toponymy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Human_geography"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Human geography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Boundaries"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Boundaries</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Cities_and_towns"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cities and towns</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Green_belt"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Green belt</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Physical_geography"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Physical geography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Geology"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Geology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Caves_and_rivers"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Caves and rivers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Levels_and_moors"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Levels and moors</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Coastline"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Coastline</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Climate"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Climate</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Economy_and_industry"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Economy and industry</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Nuclear_electricity"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Nuclear electricity</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Demography"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Demography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Politics"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Local_government"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Local government</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Civil_parishes"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Civil parishes</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Emergency_services"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Emergency services</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Transport"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Transport</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Further_and_higher_education"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Further and higher education</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">Toponymy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Toponymy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Somerset\'s name derives from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Sumorsaete" title="Sumorsaete">Sumors\xc7\xa3te</a></i>, short for <i>Sumort\xc5\xabns\xc7\xa3te</i>, meaning "the people living at or dependent on Sumort\xc5\xabn (<a href="/wiki/Somerton,_Somerset" title="Somerton, Somerset">Somerton</a>)".<sup id="cite_ref-watts1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watts1-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The first known use of <i>Somers\xc3\xa6te</i> is in the law code of <a href="/wiki/Ine_of_Wessex" title="Ine of Wessex">King Ine</a> who was the Saxon King of Wessex from 688 to 726, making Somerset along with <a href="/wiki/Hampshire" title="Hampshire">Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wiltshire" title="Wiltshire">Wiltshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorset" title="Dorset">Dorset</a> one of the oldest extant units of local government in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> An alternative suggestion is the name derives from <i>Seo-mere-saetan</i> meaning "settlers by the sea lakes".<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Old English name is used in the <a href="/wiki/Motto" title="Motto">motto</a> of the county, <i>Sumors\xc7\xa3te ealle</i>, meaning "all the people of Somerset". Adopted as the motto in 1911, the phrase is taken from the <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i>. Somerset was a part of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, and the phrase refers to the wholehearted support the people of Somerset gave to <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">King Alfred</a> in his struggle to save Wessex from <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Viking</a> invaders.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Somerset settlement names are mostly Anglo-Saxon in origin, but some hill names include <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a> Celtic elements. For example, an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_charters" title="Anglo-Saxon charters">Anglo-Saxon charter</a> of 682 refers to Creechborough Hill as "the hill the British call <i>Cructan</i> and the Anglo-Saxons call <i>Crychbeorh</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Some modern names are <a href="/wiki/British_language_(Celtic)" class="mw-redirect" title="British language (Celtic)">Brythonic</a> in origin, such as <a href="/wiki/Tarnock" class="mw-redirect" title="Tarnock">Tarnock</a>, while others have both Saxon and Brythonic elements, such as <a href="/wiki/Pen_Hill" title="Pen Hill">Pen Hill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Somerset" title="History of Somerset">History of Somerset</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg/220px-Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg/330px-Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg/440px-Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5500" data-file-height="4228" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Somerset_in_1646.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the county in 1646, author unknown</div></div></div>\n<p>The caves of the <a href="/wiki/Mendip_Hills" title="Mendip Hills">Mendip Hills</a> were settled during the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Palaeolithic</a> period,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and contain extensive archaeological sites such as those at <a href="/wiki/Cheddar_Gorge" title="Cheddar Gorge">Cheddar Gorge</a>. Bones from <a href="/wiki/Gough%27s_Cave" title="Gough&#39;s Cave">Gough\'s Cave</a> have been dated to 12,000 BC, and a complete skeleton, known as <a href="/wiki/Cheddar_Man" title="Cheddar Man">Cheddar Man</a>, dates from <a href="/wiki/8th_millennium_BC" title="8th millennium BC">7150 BC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Examples of cave art have been found in <a href="/wiki/Aveline%27s_Hole" title="Aveline&#39;s Hole">Aveline\'s Hole</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Some caves continued to be occupied until modern times, including <a href="/wiki/Wookey_Hole_Caves" title="Wookey Hole Caves">Wookey Hole</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Somerset_Levels" title="Somerset Levels">Somerset Levels</a>\xe2\x80\x94specifically <a href="/wiki/Dry_point" title="Dry point">dry points</a> at <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury" title="Glastonbury">Glastonbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brent_Knoll" title="Brent Knoll">Brent Knoll</a>\xe2\x80\x94 also have a long history of settlement, and are known to have been settled by <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> hunters.<sup id="cite_ref-Dunning_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dunning-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mesolithic_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mesolithic-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Travel in the area was facilitated by the construction of one of the world\'s oldest known engineered roadways, the <a href="/wiki/Sweet_Track" title="Sweet Track">Sweet Track</a>, which dates from 3807 BC or 3806 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;Note 1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brunning_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brunning-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The exact age of the <a href="/wiki/Henge" title="Henge">henge monument</a> at <a href="/wiki/Stanton_Drew_stone_circles" title="Stanton Drew stone circles">Stanton Drew stone circles</a> is unknown, but it is believed to be <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> There are numerous <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Hill_fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Hill fort">hill forts</a>, some of which, like <a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Castle,_Somerset" title="Cadbury Castle, Somerset">Cadbury Castle</a><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\nand <a href="/wiki/Ham_Hill,_Somerset" title="Ham Hill, Somerset">Ham Hill</a>, were later reoccupied in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the authority of the future emperor <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a>, as part of the ongoing expansion of the Roman presence in Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Augusta" title="Legio II Augusta">Second Legion Augusta</a> invaded Somerset from the south-east in AD&#160;47. The county remained part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> until around AD&#160;409, when the Roman occupation of Britain came to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-rajan_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rajan-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\nA variety of Roman remains have been found, including <a href="/wiki/Pagans_Hill_Roman_temple" title="Pagans Hill Roman temple">Pagans Hill Roman temple</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chew_Stoke" title="Chew Stoke">Chew Stoke</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-hucker_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hucker-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Low_Ham_Roman_Villa" title="Low Ham Roman Villa">Low Ham Roman Villa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Baths_(Bath)" title="Roman Baths (Bath)">Roman Baths</a> that gave their name to the city of <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pulteney_Bridge,_Bath_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Yellow/Gray stone bridge with three arches over water which reflects the bridge and the church spire behind. A weir is on the left with other yellow stone buildings behind." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pulteney_Bridge%2C_Bath_2.jpg/220px-Pulteney_Bridge%2C_Bath_2.jpg" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pulteney_Bridge%2C_Bath_2.jpg/330px-Pulteney_Bridge%2C_Bath_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Pulteney_Bridge%2C_Bath_2.jpg/440px-Pulteney_Bridge%2C_Bath_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2123" data-file-height="2999" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pulteney_Bridge,_Bath_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Palladian" class="mw-redirect" title="Palladian">Palladian</a> Pulteney Bridge at <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After the Romans left, Britain was invaded by Anglo-Saxon peoples. By AD 600 they had established control over much of what is now England, but Somerset was still in native British hands. The British held back Saxon advance into the south-west for some time longer, but by the early eighth century King <a href="/wiki/Ine_of_Wessex" title="Ine of Wessex">Ine of Wessex</a> had pushed the boundaries of the West Saxon kingdom far enough west to include Somerset.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The Saxon royal palace in <a href="/wiki/Cheddar,_Somerset" title="Cheddar, Somerset">Cheddar</a> was used several times in the 10th century to host the <a href="/wiki/Witenagemot" title="Witenagemot">Witenagemot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman Conquest</a>, the county was divided into 700 <a href="/wiki/Fiefdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiefdom">fiefs</a>, and large areas were owned by the crown,<sup id="cite_ref-EB1911_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> with fortifications such as <a href="/wiki/Dunster_Castle" title="Dunster Castle">Dunster Castle</a> used for control and defence. Somerset contains <a href="/wiki/HM_Prison_Shepton_Mallet" title="HM Prison Shepton Mallet">HM Prison Shepton Mallet</a>, which was England\'s oldest prison still in use prior to its closure in 2013, having opened in 1610.<sup id="cite_ref-Historic_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historic-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> Somerset was largely <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarian</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> with key engagements being the <a href="/wiki/Sieges_of_Taunton" title="Sieges of Taunton">Sieges of Taunton</a><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Langport" title="Battle of Langport">Battle of Langport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In 1685 the <a href="/wiki/Monmouth_Rebellion" title="Monmouth Rebellion">Monmouth Rebellion</a> was played out in Somerset and neighbouring Dorset.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The rebels landed at <a href="/wiki/Lyme_Regis" title="Lyme Regis">Lyme Regis</a> and travelled north, hoping to capture <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> and Bath, but they were defeated in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sedgemoor" title="Battle of Sedgemoor">Battle of Sedgemoor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Westonzoyland" title="Westonzoyland">Westonzoyland</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Pitched_battle" title="Pitched battle">pitched battle</a> fought in England.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Arthur Wellesley</a> took his title, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Wellington_(title)" title="Duke of Wellington (title)">Duke of Wellington</a> from the town of <a href="/wiki/Wellington,_Somerset" title="Wellington, Somerset">Wellington</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> he is commemorated on a nearby hill by a large, spotlit <a href="/wiki/Obelisk" title="Obelisk">obelisk</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Wellington_Monument,_Somerset" title="Wellington Monument, Somerset">Wellington Monument</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> in the Midlands and Northern England spelled the end for most of Somerset\'s cottage industries. Farming continued to flourish, however, and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bath_and_West_of_England_Society" title="Royal Bath and West of England Society">Bath and West of England Society for the Encouragement of Agriculture, Arts, Manufactures and Commerce</a> was founded in 1777 to improve farming methods. Despite this, 20 years later <a href="/wiki/John_Billingsley_(agriculturist)" title="John Billingsley (agriculturist)">John Billingsley</a> conducted a survey of the county\'s agriculture in 1795 and found that agricultural methods could still be improved.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Coal mining was an important industry in north Somerset during the 18th and 19th centuries, and by 1800 it was prominent in <a href="/wiki/Radstock" title="Radstock">Radstock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Somerset_Coalfield" title="Somerset Coalfield">Somerset Coalfield</a> reached its peak production by the 1920s, but all the pits have now been closed, the last in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-cornwell_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornwell-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Most of the surface buildings have been removed, and apart from a winding wheel outside <a href="/wiki/Radstock_Museum" title="Radstock Museum">Radstock Museum</a>, little evidence of their former existence remains. Further west, the <a href="/wiki/Brendon_Hills" title="Brendon Hills">Brendon Hills</a> were mined for iron ore in the late 19th century; this was taken by the <a href="/wiki/West_Somerset_Mineral_Railway" title="West Somerset Mineral Railway">West Somerset Mineral Railway</a> to <a href="/wiki/Watchet" title="Watchet">Watchet</a> Harbour for shipment to the furnaces at <a href="/wiki/Ebbw_Vale" title="Ebbw Vale">Ebbw Vale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many Somerset soldiers died during the First World War, with the <a href="/wiki/Somerset_Light_Infantry" title="Somerset Light Infantry">Somerset Light Infantry</a> suffering nearly 5,000 casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> War memorials were put up in most of the county\'s towns and villages; only nine, described as the <a href="/wiki/Thankful_Villages" title="Thankful Villages">Thankful Villages</a>, had none of their residents killed. During the Second World War the county was a base for troops preparing for the <a href="/wiki/Normandy_landings" title="Normandy landings">D-Day landings</a>. Some of the hospitals which were built for the casualties of the war remain in use. The <a href="/wiki/Taunton_Stop_Line" title="Taunton Stop Line">Taunton Stop Line</a> was set up to repel a potential German invasion. The remains of its <a href="/wiki/Bunker" title="Bunker">pill boxes</a> can still be seen along the coast, and south through <a href="/wiki/Ilminster" title="Ilminster">Ilminster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chard,_Somerset" title="Chard, Somerset">Chard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of decoy towns were constructed in Somerset in World War II to protect Bristol and other towns, at night. They were designed to mimic the geometry of "blacked out" streets, railway lines, and <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Temple_Meads_railway_station" title="Bristol Temple Meads railway station">Bristol Temple Meads railway station</a>, to encourage bombers away from these targets.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> One, on the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Beams" title="Battle of the Beams">radio beam</a> flight path to Bristol, was constructed on <a href="/wiki/Black_Down,_Somerset" title="Black Down, Somerset">Beacon Batch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> It was laid out by <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_Studios" title="Shepperton Studios">Shepperton Studios</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">aerial photographs</a> of the city\'s railway <a href="/wiki/Classification_yard" title="Classification yard">marshalling yards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The decoys were fitted with dim red lights, simulating activities like the stoking of <a href="/wiki/Steam_locomotive" title="Steam locomotive">steam locomotives</a>. Burning bales of straw soaked in creosote were used to simulate the effects of <a href="/wiki/Incendiary_device" title="Incendiary device">incendiary bombs</a> dropped by the first wave of Pathfinder night bombers; meanwhile, incendiary bombs dropped on the correct location were quickly smothered, wherever possible. Drums of oil were also ignited to simulate the effect of a blazing city or town, with the aim of fooling subsequent waves of bombers into dropping their bombs on the wrong location.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chew_Magna" title="Chew Magna">Chew Magna</a> decoy town was hit by half a <a href="/wiki/Dozen" title="Dozen">dozen</a> bombs on 2 December 1940, and over a thousand incendiaries on 3 January 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The following night the <a href="/wiki/Uphill" title="Uphill">Uphill</a> decoy town, protecting <a href="/wiki/Weston-super-Mare" title="Weston-super-Mare">Weston-super-Mare</a>\'s airfield, was bombed; a herd of <a href="/wiki/Dairy_cattle" title="Dairy cattle">dairy cows</a> was hit, killing some and severely injuring others.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_geography">Human geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Human geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boundaries">Boundaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Boundaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg/220px-Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg/330px-Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg/440px-Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bristol_MMB_%C2%ABD0_Avon_Gorge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Avon_Gorge" title="Avon Gorge">Avon Gorge</a>, the historic boundary between Gloucestershire and Somerset, and also <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>; Somerset is to the left</div></div></div>\n<p>The boundaries of Somerset are very similar to how they were in medieval times. They have been largely unaltered. The <a href="/wiki/River_Avon_(Bristol)" class="mw-redirect" title="River Avon (Bristol)">River Avon</a> formed much of the border with Gloucestershire, except that the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_(county_division)" title="Hundred (county division)">hundred</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bath_Forum" title="Bath Forum">Bath Forum</a>, which straddles the Avon, formed part of Somerset. Bristol began as a town on the Gloucestershire side of the Avon, however as it grew it extended across the river into Somerset. In 1373 <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> proclaimed "that the town of Bristol with its suburbs and precincts shall henceforth be separate from the counties of Gloucester and Somerset&#160;... and that it should be a county by itself".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The present-day northern border of Somerset (adjoining the counties of Bristol and Gloucestershire) runs along the southern bank of the Avon from the Bristol Channel, then follows around the southern edge of the Bristol built-up area, before continuing upstream along the Avon, and then diverges from the river to include Bath and its historic hinterland to the north of the Avon, before meeting Wiltshire at the <i>Three Shire Stones</i> on the <i><a href="/wiki/Fosse_Way" title="Fosse Way">Fosse Way</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Batheaston" title="Batheaston">Batheaston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ordnances_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ordnances-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cities_and_towns">Cities and towns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cities and towns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_places_in_Somerset" title="List of places in Somerset">List of places in Somerset</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_settlements_in_Somerset_by_population" title="List of settlements in Somerset by population">List of settlements in Somerset by population</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Category:Populated_places_in_Somerset" title="Category:Populated places in Somerset">Category:Populated places in Somerset</a></div>\n<p>Somerton took over from <a href="/wiki/Ilchester" title="Ilchester">Ilchester</a> as the <a href="/wiki/County_town" title="County town">county town</a> in the late thirteenth century,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> but it declined in importance and the status of county town transferred to Taunton about 1366.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The county has two <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">cities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wells,_Somerset" title="Wells, Somerset">Wells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Category:Towns_in_Somerset" title="Category:Towns in Somerset">30 towns</a> (including the county town of Taunton, which has no town council but instead is the chief settlement of the county\'s only extant <a href="/wiki/Borough_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Borough status in the United Kingdom">borough</a>). The largest urban areas in terms of population are Bath, Weston-super-Mare, <a href="/wiki/Taunton" title="Taunton">Taunton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yeovil" title="Yeovil">Yeovil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bridgwater" title="Bridgwater">Bridgwater</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Many settlements developed because of their strategic importance in relation to geographical features, such as river crossings or valleys in ranges of hills. Examples include Axbridge on the <a href="/wiki/River_Axe_(Bristol_Channel)" title="River Axe (Bristol Channel)">River Axe</a>, Castle Cary on the <a href="/wiki/River_Cary" class="mw-redirect" title="River Cary">River Cary</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Petherton" title="North Petherton">North Petherton</a> on the <a href="/wiki/River_Parrett" title="River Parrett">River Parrett</a>, and Ilminster, where there was a crossing point on the <a href="/wiki/River_Isle" title="River Isle">River Isle</a>. Midsomer Norton lies on the <a href="/wiki/River_Somer" title="River Somer">River Somer</a>; while the <a href="/wiki/Wellow_Brook" title="Wellow Brook">Wellow Brook</a> and the <i>Fosse Way</i> <a href="/wiki/Roman_roads_in_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman roads in Britain">Roman road</a> run through Radstock. <a href="/wiki/Chard,_Somerset" title="Chard, Somerset">Chard</a> is the most southerly town in Somerset and one of the highest, though at an altitude of 126&#160;m (413&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Wiveliscombe" title="Wiveliscombe">Wiveliscombe</a> is the highest town in the county.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_belt">Green belt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Green belt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Avon_Green_Belt" title="Avon Green Belt">Avon Green Belt</a></div>\n<p>The county contains several miles wide sections of the Avon <a href="/wiki/Green_belt" title="Green belt">green belt</a> area, which is primarily in place to prevent urban sprawl from the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Built-up_Area" title="Bristol Built-up Area">Bristol</a> and Bath built up areas into the rural areas of North Somerset<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>, Bath and North East Somerset<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>, and Mendip<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> districts in the county, as well as maintaining surrounding countryside. It stretches from the coastline between the towns of Portishead and Clevedon, extending eastwards past <a href="/wiki/Nailsea" title="Nailsea">Nailsea</a>, around the Bristol conurbation, and through to the city of Bath. The green belt border intersects with the Mendip Hills Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty (AONB) along its south boundary, and meets the Cotswolds AONB by its eastern extent along the Wiltshire county border, creating an extended area protected from inappropriate development.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_geography">Physical geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Physical geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Somerset" title="Geography of Somerset">Geography of Somerset</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Somerset" title="Geology of Somerset">Geology of Somerset</a></div>\n<p>Much of the landscape of Somerset falls into types determined by the underlying geology. These landscapes are the <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> <a href="/wiki/Karst" title="Karst">karst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Jurassic" title="Early Jurassic">lias</a> of the north, the <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">vales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> of the centre, the <a href="/wiki/Oolite" title="Oolite">oolites</a> of the east and south, and the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> of the west.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uk_som_brue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Long straight water filled channel, with occasional trees on the left hand bank and grass on the right hand bank." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uk_som_brue.jpg/220px-Uk_som_brue.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uk_som_brue.jpg/330px-Uk_som_brue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uk_som_brue.jpg/440px-Uk_som_brue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uk_som_brue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/River_Brue" title="River Brue">River Brue</a> in an artificial channel draining farmland near <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury" title="Glastonbury">Glastonbury</a></div></div></div>\n<p>To the north-east of the Somerset Levels, the Mendip Hills are moderately high limestone hills. The central and western Mendip Hills was designated an <a href="/wiki/Area_of_Outstanding_Natural_Beauty" title="Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty">Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty</a> in 1972 and covers 198&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (76&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The main <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> on these hills is <a href="/wiki/Calcareous_grassland" title="Calcareous grassland">calcareous grassland</a>, with some <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable</a> agriculture. To the south-west of the Somerset Levels are the <a href="/wiki/Quantock_Hills" title="Quantock Hills">Quantock Hills</a> which was England\'s first Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty designated in 1956<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> which is covered in heathland, oak woodlands, ancient parklands with plantations of conifer and covers 99 square kilometres. The Somerset Coalfield is part of a larger coalfield which stretches into <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>. To the north of the Mendip hills is the <a href="/wiki/Chew_Valley" title="Chew Valley">Chew Valley</a> and to the south, on the clay substrate, are broad valleys which support dairy farming and drain into the Somerset Levels.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caves_and_rivers">Caves and rivers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Caves and rivers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There is an extensive network of <a href="/wiki/Caves_of_the_Mendip_Hills" title="Caves of the Mendip Hills">caves</a>, including Wookey Hole, underground rivers, and <a href="/wiki/Canyon" title="Canyon">gorges</a>, including the Cheddar Gorge and <a href="/wiki/Ebbor_Gorge" title="Ebbor Gorge">Ebbor Gorge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The county has many rivers, including the Axe, <a href="/wiki/River_Brue" title="River Brue">Brue</a>, Cary, Parrett, <a href="/wiki/River_Sheppey" title="River Sheppey">Sheppey</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Tone" title="River Tone">Tone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congresbury_Yeo" title="Congresbury Yeo">Yeo</a>. These both feed and drain the flat levels and moors of mid and west Somerset.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the north of the county the <a href="/wiki/River_Chew" title="River Chew">River Chew</a> flows into the <a href="/wiki/River_Avon_(Bristol)" class="mw-redirect" title="River Avon (Bristol)">Bristol Avon</a>. The Parrett is tidal almost to <a href="/wiki/Langport" title="Langport">Langport</a>, where there is evidence of two Roman wharfs.<sup id="cite_ref-hadfield_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hadfield-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> At the same site during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">King Charles I</a>, river tolls were levied on boats to pay for the maintenance of the bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-hadfield_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hadfield-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_and_moors">Levels and moors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Levels and moors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glastonbury_(part_of)_from_the_tor_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Glastonbury_%28part_of%29_from_the_tor_arp.jpg/220px-Glastonbury_%28part_of%29_from_the_tor_arp.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Glastonbury_%28part_of%29_from_the_tor_arp.jpg/330px-Glastonbury_%28part_of%29_from_the_tor_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Glastonbury_%28part_of%29_from_the_tor_arp.jpg/440px-Glastonbury_%28part_of%29_from_the_tor_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1070" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glastonbury_(part_of)_from_the_tor_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The town of Glastonbury looking west from the top of Glastonbury Tor. The fields in the distance are the Somerset Levels.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Somerset Levels (or Somerset Levels and Moors as they are less commonly but more correctly known) are a sparsely populated <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> area of central Somerset, between the Quantock and Mendip hills. They consist of marine clay levels along the coast, and the inland (often <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> based) moors. The Levels are divided into two by the <a href="/wiki/Polden_Hills" title="Polden Hills">Polden Hills</a>; land to the south is drained by the <a href="/wiki/River_Parrett" title="River Parrett">River Parrett</a> while land to the north is drained by the River Axe and the River Brue. The total area of the Levels amounts to about 647.5 square kilometres (160,000 acres)<sup id="cite_ref-robinwilliams_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robinwilliams-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\nand broadly corresponds to the administrative district of <a href="/wiki/Sedgemoor" title="Sedgemoor">Sedgemoor</a> but also includes the south west of <a href="/wiki/Mendip_District" title="Mendip District">Mendip</a> district. Approximately 70% of the area is grassland and 30% is arable.<sup id="cite_ref-robinwilliams_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robinwilliams-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\nStretching about 32 kilometres (20&#160;mi) inland, this expanse of flat land barely rises above sea level. Before it was drained, much of the land was under a shallow <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">brackish sea</a> in winter and was <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marsh land</a> in summer. Drainage began with the Romans, and was restarted at various times: by the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>; in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Abbey" title="Glastonbury Abbey">Glastonbury Abbey</a>, during 1400\xe2\x80\x931770; and during the Second World War, with the construction of the <a href="/wiki/River_Huntspill" title="River Huntspill">Huntspill River</a>. Pumping and management of water levels still continues.<sup id="cite_ref-williams_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williams-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three small brown horses on grassy area. In the distance are hills." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg/220px-Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg" width="220" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg/330px-Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg/440px-Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1700" data-file-height="769" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exmoors_on_Exmoor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Exmoor landscape with the native Exmoor Pony</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Somerset_Levels" title="North Somerset Levels">North Somerset Levels</a> basin, north of the Mendips, covers a smaller geographical area than the Somerset Levels; and forms a coastal area around <a href="/wiki/Avonmouth" title="Avonmouth">Avonmouth</a>. It too was reclaimed by draining.<sup id="cite_ref-williams_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williams-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rippon_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rippon-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> It is mirrored, across the <a href="/wiki/Severn_Estuary" title="Severn Estuary">Severn Estuary</a>, in Wales, by a similar low-lying area: the <a href="/wiki/Caldicot_and_Wentloog_Levels" title="Caldicot and Wentloog Levels">Caldicot and Wentloog Levels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rippon_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rippon-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the far west of the county, running into Devon, is <a href="/wiki/Exmoor" title="Exmoor">Exmoor</a>, a high Devonian sandstone <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moor</a>, which was designated as a <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national park</a> in 1954, under the 1949 <a href="/wiki/National_Parks_and_Access_to_the_Countryside_Act_1949" title="National Parks and Access to the Countryside Act 1949">National Parks and Access to the Countryside Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\nThe highest point in Somerset is <a href="/wiki/Dunkery_Hill" title="Dunkery Hill">Dunkery Beacon</a> on Exmoor, with an altitude of 519 metres (1,703 feet).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over 100 sites in Somerset have been designated as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sites_of_Special_Scientific_Interest_in_Somerset" title="List of Sites of Special Scientific Interest in Somerset">Sites of Special Scientific Interest</a>.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_hills_of_Somerset" title="List of hills of Somerset">List of hills of Somerset</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastline">Coastline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Coastline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Green covered rocky land in expanse of sea. Hills behind." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg/220px-Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg/330px-Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg/440px-Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brean_Down_from_Steepholm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_93827.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Brean_Down" title="Brean Down">Brean Down</a> from Steep Holm</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg" class="image"><img alt="small boats lined up in harbour. Crane in the background &amp; metal walkway in the foreground." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg/170px-Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg/255px-Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg/340px-Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3872" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Watchetmarinab%26w.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The marina in <a href="/wiki/Watchet" title="Watchet">Watchet</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The 64&#160;km (40&#160;mi) coastline of the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Channel" title="Bristol Channel">Bristol Channel</a> and Severn Estuary forms part of the northern border of Somerset.<sup id="cite_ref-dandsfire_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dandsfire-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\nThe Bristol Channel has the second largest <a href="/wiki/Tidal_range" title="Tidal range">tidal range</a> in the world. At <a href="/wiki/Burnham-on-Sea" title="Burnham-on-Sea">Burnham-on-Sea</a>, for example, the tidal range of a spring tide is more than 12 metres (39 feet).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\nProposals for the construction of a <a href="/wiki/Severn_Barrage" title="Severn Barrage">Severn Barrage</a> aim to harness this energy. The island of <a href="/wiki/Steep_Holm" title="Steep Holm">Steep Holm</a> in the Bristol Channel is within the ceremonial county and is now administered by North Somerset Council.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main coastal towns are, from the west to the north-east, <a href="/wiki/Minehead" title="Minehead">Minehead</a>, Watchet, Burnham-on-Sea, Weston-super-Mare, <a href="/wiki/Clevedon" title="Clevedon">Clevedon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portishead,_Somerset" title="Portishead, Somerset">Portishead</a>. The coastal area between Minehead and the eastern extreme of the administrative county\'s coastline at <a href="/wiki/Brean_Down" title="Brean Down">Brean Down</a> is known as <a href="/wiki/Bridgwater_Bay" title="Bridgwater Bay">Bridgwater Bay</a>, and is a <a href="/wiki/National_nature_reserves_in_England" title="National nature reserves in England">National Nature Reserve</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\nNorth of that, the coast forms <a href="/wiki/Weston_Bay" title="Weston Bay">Weston Bay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sand_Bay" title="Sand Bay">Sand Bay</a> whose northern tip, <a href="/wiki/Sand_Point_and_Middle_Hope" title="Sand Point and Middle Hope">Sand Point</a>, marks the lower limit of the Severn Estuary.<sup id="cite_ref-osgb_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osgb-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the mid and north of the county the coastline is low as the level wetlands of the levels meet the sea. In the west, the coastline is high and dramatic where the <a href="/wiki/Plateau" title="Plateau">plateau</a> of Exmoor meets the sea, with high cliffs and waterfalls.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Along with the rest of <a href="/wiki/South_West_England" title="South West England">South West England</a>, Somerset has a temperate climate which is generally wetter and milder than the rest of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-weather_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weather-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The annual mean temperature is approximately 10&#160;\xc2\xb0C (50.0&#160;\xc2\xb0F). <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">Seasonal temperature variation</a> is less extreme than most of the United Kingdom because of the adjacent sea temperatures. The summer months of July and August are the warmest with mean daily maxima of approximately 21&#160;\xc2\xb0C (69.8&#160;\xc2\xb0F). In winter mean minimum temperatures of 1&#160;\xc2\xb0C (33.8&#160;\xc2\xb0F) or 2&#160;\xc2\xb0C (35.6&#160;\xc2\xb0F) are common.<sup id="cite_ref-weather_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weather-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In the summer the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a> high pressure affects the south-west of England, but <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convective</a> cloud sometimes forms inland, reducing the number of hours of sunshine. Annual sunshine rates are slightly less than the regional average of 1,600&#160;hours.<sup id="cite_ref-weather_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weather-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In December 1998 there were 20 days without sun recorded at Yeovilton. Most of the rainfall in the south-west is caused by <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">Atlantic depressions</a> or by convection. Most of the rainfall in autumn and winter is caused by the Atlantic depressions, which is when they are most active. In summer, a large proportion of the rainfall is caused by sun heating the ground leading to convection and to showers and thunderstorms. Average rainfall is around 700&#160;mm (28&#160;in). About 8\xe2\x80\x9315 days of snowfall is typical. November to March have the highest mean wind speeds, and June to August the lightest winds. The predominant wind direction is from the south-west.<sup id="cite_ref-weather_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weather-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%;
