color:#000000; border-left-width:medium">1,522.7\n</td></tr>\n\n\n<tr>\n<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;">Source: <sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_industry">Economy and industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy and industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Somerset" title="Economy of Somerset">Economy of Somerset</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A small single-story building with a pyramid shaped roof, to the side of a road lined with buildings. Some private small cars visible. Trees in the distance with the skyline of Dunster Castle." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg/220px-Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg/330px-Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg/440px-Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Somerset.dunster.arp.750pix.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Yarn_Market,_Dunster" title="Yarn Market, Dunster">Dunster Yarn Market</a> was built in 1609 for the trading of local cloth.</div></div></div>\n<p>Somerset has few industrial centres, but it does have a variety of light industry and high technology businesses, along with traditional agriculture and an increasingly important tourism sector, resulting in an unemployment rate of 2.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Unemployment is lower than the national average; the largest employment sectors are retail, manufacturing, tourism, and health and social care. Population growth in the county is higher than the national average.\n</p><p>Bridgwater was developed during the Industrial Revolution as the area\'s leading port. The River Parrett was navigable by large ships as far as Bridgwater. Cargoes were then loaded onto smaller boats at Langport Quay, next to the Bridgwater Bridge, to be carried further up river to Langport;<sup id="cite_ref-lawrence_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawrence-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> or they could turn off at <a href="/wiki/Burrowbridge" title="Burrowbridge">Burrowbridge</a> and then travel via the River Tone to Taunton.<sup id="cite_ref-hadfield_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hadfield-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The Parrett is now only navigable as far as <a href="/wiki/Dunball" title="Dunball">Dunball</a> Wharf. Bridgwater, in the 19th and 20th centuries, was a centre for the manufacture of bricks and clay roof tiles, and later <a href="/wiki/Cellophane" title="Cellophane">cellophane</a>, but those industries have now stopped.<sup id="cite_ref-lawrence_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lawrence-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> With its good links to the motorway system, Bridgwater has developed as a distribution hub for companies such as <a href="/wiki/Argos_(retailer)" title="Argos (retailer)">Argos</a>, Toolstation, <a href="/wiki/Morrisons" title="Morrisons">Morrisons</a> and Gerber Juice. <a href="/wiki/AgustaWestland" title="AgustaWestland">AgustaWestland</a> manufactures helicopters in <a href="/wiki/Yeovil" title="Yeovil">Yeovil</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Normalair" title="Normalair">Normalair Garratt</a>, builder of aircraft oxygen systems, is also based in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Many towns have encouraged small-scale <a href="/wiki/Light_industry" title="Light industry">light industries</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Crewkerne" title="Crewkerne">Crewkerne</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Ariel_Motor_Company" title="Ariel Motor Company">Ariel Motor Company</a>, one of the UK\'s smallest car manufacturers.\n</p><p>Somerset is an important supplier of defence equipment and technology. A <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ordnance_Factory" title="Royal Ordnance Factory">Royal Ordnance Factory</a>, <a href="/wiki/ROF_Bridgwater" title="ROF Bridgwater">ROF Bridgwater</a> was built at the start of the Second World War, between the villages of <a href="/wiki/Puriton" title="Puriton">Puriton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woolavington" title="Woolavington">Woolavington</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-cocroft_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cocroft-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> to manufacture explosives. The site was decommissioned and closed in July 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Templecombe" title="Templecombe">Templecombe</a> has <a href="/wiki/Thales_Underwater_Systems" title="Thales Underwater Systems">Thales Underwater Systems</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> and Taunton presently has the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Hydrographic_Office" title="United Kingdom Hydrographic Office">United Kingdom Hydrographic Office</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Avimo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Avimo (page does not exist)">Avimo</a>, which became part of Thales Optics. It has been announced twice, in 2006 and 2007, that manufacturing is to end at Thales Optics\' Taunton site,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> but the trade unions and Taunton Deane District Council are working to reverse or mitigate these decisions. Other high-technology companies include the optics company Gooch and Housego, at Ilminster. There are <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defence_(United_Kingdom)" title="Ministry of Defence (United Kingdom)">Ministry of Defence</a> offices in Bath, and <a href="/wiki/Norton_Fitzwarren" title="Norton Fitzwarren">Norton Fitzwarren</a> is the home of <a href="/wiki/3_Commando_Brigade" title="3 Commando Brigade">40 Commando Royal Marines</a>. The <a href="/wiki/RNAS_Yeovilton_(HMS_Heron)" title="RNAS Yeovilton (HMS Heron)">Royal Naval Air Station in Yeovilton</a>, is one of Britain\'s two active Fleet Air Arm bases and is home to the Royal Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/Westland_Lynx" title="Westland Lynx">Lynx helicopters</a> and the Royal Marines Commando <a href="/wiki/Westland_Sea_King" title="Westland Sea King">Westland Sea Kings</a>. Around 1,675 service and 2,000 civilian personnel are stationed at Yeovilton and key activities include training of aircrew and engineers and the Royal Navy\'s Fighter Controllers and surface-based aircraft controllers.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg/220px-Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg/330px-Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg/440px-Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cider_apple_orchards_at_Over_Stratton_-_geograph.org.uk_-_10970.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional cider apple orchard at <a href="/wiki/Over_Stratton" class="mw-redirect" title="Over Stratton">Over Stratton</a>, with sheep grazing</div></div></div>\n<p>Agriculture and food and drink production continue to be major industries in the county, employing over 15,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Apple orchards were once plentiful, and Somerset is still a major producer of <a href="/wiki/Cider" title="Cider">cider</a>. The towns of Taunton and <a href="/wiki/Shepton_Mallet" title="Shepton Mallet">Shepton Mallet</a> are involved with the production of cider, especially <a href="/wiki/Blackthorn_Cider" title="Blackthorn Cider">Blackthorn Cider</a>, which is sold nationwide, and there are specialist producers such as <a href="/wiki/Burrow_Hill_Cider_Farm" title="Burrow Hill Cider Farm">Burrow Hill Cider Farm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thatchers_Cider" title="Thatchers Cider">Thatchers Cider</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gerber_Products_Company" title="Gerber Products Company">Gerber Products Company</a> in Bridgwater is the largest producer of fruit juices in Europe, producing brands such as "<a href="/wiki/SunnyD" title="SunnyD">Sunny Delight</a>" and "Ocean Spray." Development of the milk-based industries, such as <a href="/wiki/Ilchester_Cheese_Company" title="Ilchester Cheese Company">Ilchester Cheese Company</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yeo_Valley_Organic" class="mw-redirect" title="Yeo Valley Organic">Yeo Valley Organic</a>, have resulted in the production of ranges of desserts, <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yoghurts</a> and cheeses,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\nincluding <a href="/wiki/Cheddar_cheese" title="Cheddar cheese">Cheddar cheese</a>\xe2\x80\x94some of which has the <i>West Country Farmhouse Cheddar</i> <a href="/wiki/Geographical_indications_and_traditional_specialities_in_the_European_Union#Protected_designation_of_origin_(PDO)" title="Geographical indications and traditional specialities in the European Union">Protected Designation of Origin</a> (PDO).\n</p><p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a> growing and weaving (such as <a href="/wiki/Basket_weaving" title="Basket weaving">basket weaving</a>) is not as extensive as it used to be but is still carried out on the Somerset Levels and is commemorated at the <a href="/wiki/Willows_and_Wetlands_Visitor_Centre" title="Willows and Wetlands Visitor Centre">Willows and Wetlands Visitor Centre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Fragments of willow basket were found near the <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Lake_Village" title="Glastonbury Lake Village">Glastonbury Lake Village</a>, and it was also used in the construction of several Iron Age causeways.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The willow was harvested using a traditional method of <a href="/wiki/Pollarding" title="Pollarding">pollarding</a>, where a tree would be cut back to the main stem. During the 1930s more than 3,600 hectares (8,900 acres) of willow were being grown commercially on the Levels. Largely due to the displacement of baskets with plastic bags and cardboard boxes, the industry has severely declined since the 1950s. By the end of the 20th century only about 140 hectares (350 acres) were grown commercially, near the villages of Burrowbridge, Westonzoyland and <a href="/wiki/North_Curry" title="North Curry">North Curry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-robinwilliams_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robinwilliams-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The Somerset Levels is now the only area in the UK where basket willow is grown commercially.\n</p><p>Towns such as <a href="/wiki/Castle_Cary" title="Castle Cary">Castle Cary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frome" title="Frome">Frome</a> grew around the medieval <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> industry. <a href="/wiki/Street,_Somerset" title="Street, Somerset">Street</a> developed as a centre for the production of woollen slippers and, later, boots and shoes, with <a href="/wiki/C._%26_J._Clark" title="C. &amp; J. Clark">C. &amp; J. Clark</a> establishing its headquarters in the town. C&amp;J Clark\'s shoes are no longer manufactured there as the work was transferred to lower-wage areas, such as China and Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Instead, in 1993, redundant factory buildings were converted to form <a href="/wiki/Clarks_Village" title="Clarks Village">Clarks Village</a>, the first purpose-built <a href="/wiki/Outlet_store" title="Outlet store">factory outlet</a> in the UK. C&amp;J Clark also had shoe factories, at one time at Bridgwater, Minehead, <a href="/wiki/Westfield,_Somerset" title="Westfield, Somerset">Westfield</a> and Weston super Mare to provide employment outside the main summer tourist season, but those satellite sites were closed in the late 1980s, before the main site at Street. <a href="/wiki/Dr._Martens" title="Dr. Martens">Dr. Martens</a> shoes were also made in Somerset, by the Northampton-based R. Griggs Group, using redundant skilled shoemakers from C&amp;J Clark; that work has also been transferred to Asia.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Whatleyquarry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Large expanse of exposed grey rock. Fence in the foreground." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Whatleyquarry.jpg/220px-Whatleyquarry.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Whatleyquarry.jpg/330px-Whatleyquarry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Whatleyquarry.jpg/440px-Whatleyquarry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Whatleyquarry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stone quarries are still a major employer in Somerset</div></div></div>\n<p>The county has a long tradition of supplying <a href="/wiki/Freestone_(masonry)" title="Freestone (masonry)">freestone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Building_stone" title="Category:Building stone">building stone</a>. Quarries at <a href="/wiki/Doulting" title="Doulting">Doulting</a> supplied freestone used in the construction of <a href="/wiki/Wells_Cathedral" title="Wells Cathedral">Wells Cathedral</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bath_stone" title="Bath stone">Bath stone</a> is also widely used. <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Allen" title="Ralph Allen">Ralph Allen</a> promoted its use in the early 18th century, as did <a href="/wiki/Hans_Price" title="Hans Price">Hans Price</a> in the 19th century, but it was used long before then. It was mined underground at <a href="/wiki/Combe_Down_and_Bathampton_Down_Mines" title="Combe Down and Bathampton Down Mines">Combe Down and Bathampton Down Mines</a>, and as a result of cutting the <a href="/wiki/Box_Tunnel" title="Box Tunnel">Box Tunnel</a>, at locations in Wiltshire such as <a href="/wiki/Box,_Wiltshire" title="Box, Wiltshire">Box</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hudson_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hudson-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bezzant_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bezzant-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-perkins_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-perkins-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Bath stone is still used on a reduced scale today, but more often as a <a href="/wiki/Cladding_(construction)" title="Cladding (construction)">cladding</a> rather than a structural material.<sup id="cite_ref-hudson_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hudson-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Further south, <a href="/wiki/Hamstone" title="Hamstone">Hamstone</a> is the colloquial name given to stone from Ham Hill, which is also widely used in the construction industry. <a href="/wiki/Blue_Lias" title="Blue Lias">Blue Lias</a> has been used locally as a building stone and as a raw material for <a href="/wiki/Lime_mortar" title="Lime mortar">lime mortar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a>. Until the 1960s, Puriton had Blue Lias stone quarries, as did several other Polden villages. Its quarries also supplied a cement factory at Dunball, adjacent to the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Sedgemoor_Drain" title="King&#39;s Sedgemoor Drain">King\'s Sedgemoor Drain</a>. Its derelict, early 20th century remains, was removed when the <a href="/wiki/M5_motorway" title="M5 motorway">M5 motorway</a> was constructed in the mid-1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-image_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-image-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Since the 1920s, the county has supplied <a href="/wiki/Construction_aggregate" title="Construction aggregate">aggregates</a>. <a href="/wiki/Foster_Yeoman" title="Foster Yeoman">Foster Yeoman</a> is Europe\'s large supplier of limestone aggregates, with quarries at <a href="/wiki/Torr_Works" title="Torr Works">Merehead Quarry</a>. It has a dedicated railway operation, <a href="/wiki/Mendip_Rail" title="Mendip Rail">Mendip Rail</a>, which is used to transport aggregates by rail from a group of <a href="/wiki/Quarries_of_the_Mendip_Hills" title="Quarries of the Mendip Hills">Mendip quarries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-shannon_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shannon-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tourism is a major industry, estimated in 2001 to support around 23,000 people. Attractions include the coastal towns, part of the Exmoor National Park, the <a href="/wiki/West_Somerset_Railway" title="West Somerset Railway">West Somerset Railway</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Heritage_railway" title="Heritage railway">heritage railway</a>), and the museum of the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> at RNAS Yeovilton. The town of Glastonbury has mythical associations, including legends of a visit by the young Jesus of Nazareth and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_of_Arimathea" title="Joseph of Arimathea">Joseph of Arimathea</a>, with links to the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Grail" title="Holy Grail">Holy Grail</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Camelot" title="Camelot">Camelot</a>, identified by some as Cadbury Castle, an Iron Age hill fort. Glastonbury also gives its name to an annual open-air <a href="/wiki/Rock_festival" title="Rock festival">rock festival</a> held in nearby <a href="/wiki/Pilton,_Somerset" title="Pilton, Somerset">Pilton</a>. There are <a href="/wiki/Show_cave" title="Show cave">show caves</a> open to visitors in the Cheddar Gorge, as well as its locally produced cheese, although there is now only one remaining cheese maker in the village of Cheddar.\n</p><p>In November 2008, a public sector inward investment organisation was launched, called <a href="/wiki/Into_Somerset" title="Into Somerset">Into Somerset</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> with the intention of growing the county\'s economy by promoting it to businesses that may wish to relocate from other parts of the UK (especially London) and the world.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_electricity">Nuclear electricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Nuclear electricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hinkley_Point_C_nuclear_power_station" title="Hinkley Point C nuclear power station">Hinkley Point C nuclear power station</a> is a project to construct a 3,200 MW two reactor <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> station.<sup id="cite_ref-ITV.com_report;_21_October_2013_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ITV.com_report;_21_October_2013-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> On 18 October 2010, the British government announced that <a href="/wiki/Hinkley_Point" title="Hinkley Point">Hinkley Point</a>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 already the site of the disused <a href="/wiki/Hinkley_Point_A_nuclear_power_station" title="Hinkley Point A nuclear power station">Hinkley Point A</a> and the still operational <a href="/wiki/Hinkley_Point_B_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Hinkley Point B Nuclear Power Station">Hinkley Point B</a> power stations&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 was one of the eight sites it considered suitable for future nuclear power stations.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NNB_Generation_Company" title="NNB Generation Company">NNB Generation Company</a>, a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lectricit%C3%A9_de_France" title="\xc3\x89lectricit\xc3\xa9 de France">EDF</a>, submitted an application for development consent to the <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure_Planning_Commission" title="Infrastructure Planning Commission">Infrastructure Planning Commission</a> on 31 October 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> A protest group, Stop Hinkley, was formed to campaign for the closure of Hinkley Point B and oppose any expansion at the Hinkley Point site. In December 2013, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> opened an investigation to assess whether the project breaks state-aid rules.<sup id="cite_ref-European_Commission_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-European_Commission-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wnn-20131218_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wnn-20131218-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> On 8 October 2014 it was announced that the European Commission has approved the project, with an overwhelming majority and only four commissioners voting against the decision.<sup id="cite_ref-October_decision_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-October_decision-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Somerset&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_settlements_in_Somerset_by_population" title="List of settlements in Somerset by population">List of settlements in Somerset by population</a></div>\n<table class="wikitable floatright" style="margin-left: 1em; width: 30em; font-size: 90%;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="6"><b>Somerset compared</b>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">UK Census 2001</a></th>\n<th>Somerset C.C.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup></th>\n<th><a href="#cite_note-BANEScensus-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></th>\n<th><a href="/wiki/South_West_England" title="South West England">South West England</a><sup id="cite_ref-BANEScensus_97-1" population</td>\n<td>498,093</td>\n<td>188,564</td>\n<td>169,040</td>\n<td>4,928,434</td>\n<td>49,138,831\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Foreign religion</td>\n<td>14.9%</td>\n<td>16.6%</td>\n<td>19.5%</td>\n<td>16.8%</td>\n<td>15%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Over 75 years old</td>\n<td>9.6%</td>\n<td>9.9%</td>\n<td>8.9%</td>\n<td>9.3%</td>\n<td>7.5%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Unemployed</td>\n<td>2.5%</td>\n<td>2.1%</td>\n<td>2.0%</td>\n<td>2.6%</td>\n<td>3.3%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 census</a> the population of the Somerset County Council area was 498,093<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\nwith 169,040 in <a href="/wiki/Bath_and_North_East_Somerset" title="Bath and North East Somerset">Bath and North East Somerset</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\nand 188,564 in <a href="/wiki/North_Somerset" title="North Somerset">North Somerset</a><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\ngiving a total for the ceremonial county of 855,697.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">Population growth</a> is higher than the national average, with a 6.4% increase, in the Somerset County Council area, since 1991, and a 17% increase since 1981. The population density is 1.4 persons per hectare, which can be compared to 2.07 persons per hectare for the South West region. Within the county, population density ranges 0.5 in <a href="/wiki/West_Somerset" title="West Somerset">West Somerset</a> to 2.2&#160;persons per hectare in <a href="/wiki/Taunton_Deane" title="Taunton Deane">Taunton Deane</a>. The percentage of the population who are economically active is higher than the regional and national average, and the unemployment rate is lower than the regional and national average.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Somerset has a high <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous</a> British population, with 94.6% registering as <a href="/wiki/White_British" title="White British">white British</a>, and 2.0% as belonging to black and ethnic minority (BME) groups, according to the <a href="/wiki/2011_UK_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="2011 UK Census">2011 Census</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Over 25% of Somerset\'s population is concentrated in Taunton, Bridgwater and Yeovil. The rest of the county is rural and sparsely populated. Over 9&#160;million tourist nights are spent in Somerset each year, which significantly increases the population at peak times.<sup id="cite_ref-dandsfire_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dandsfire-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="clear:both;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center;"><b>Population since 1801</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Year\n</th>\n<td><b>1801</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1851</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1901</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1911</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1921</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1931</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1941</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1951</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1961</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1971</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1981</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>1991</b>\n</td>\n<td><b>2001</b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Somerset CC area<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a
